<p>The <b>Maya civilization</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>) was a <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerican</a> <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> developed by the <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">Maya peoples</a>, and noted for <a href="/wiki/Maya_script" title="Maya script">its logosyllabic script</a>—the most sophisticated and highly developed <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing system</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>—as well as for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_art" title="Maya art">its art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maya_architecture" title="Maya architecture">architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maya_numerals" title="Maya numerals">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maya_calendar" title="Maya calendar">calendar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maya_astronomy" title="Maya astronomy">astronomical</a> system. The Maya civilization developed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_Region" title="Maya Region">Maya Region</a>, an area that today comprises southeastern <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, all of <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>, and the western portions of <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> and <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>. It includes the <a href="/wiki/Mayan_Lowlands" title="Mayan Lowlands">northern lowlands</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayan_Highlands" title="Mayan Highlands">highlands</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Madre_de_Chiapas" title="Sierra Madre de Chiapas">Sierra Madre</a>, the Mexican state of <a href="/wiki/Chiapas" title="Chiapas">Chiapas</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, El Salvador, and the southern lowlands of the Pacific littoral plain. "Maya" is a modern term used to refer collectively to the various peoples that inhabited this area. They did not call themselves "Maya" and did not have a sense of common identity or political unity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Today, their descendants, known collectively as the Maya, number well over 6 million individuals, speak more than twenty-eight surviving Mayan languages, and reside in nearly the same area as their ancestors.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology#Archaic_Era" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Archaic period</a>, before 2000 BC, saw the first developments in agriculture and the earliest villages. The Preclassic period (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 2000 BC to 250 AD</span>) saw the establishment of the first complex societies in the Maya region, and the cultivation of the staple crops of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_cuisine" title="Maya cuisine">Maya diet</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phaseolus_vulgaris" title="Phaseolus vulgaris">beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cucurbita" title="Cucurbita">squashes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chili peppers</a>. The first <a href="/wiki/Maya_city" title="Maya city">Maya cities</a> developed around 750 BC, and by 500 BC these cities possessed monumental architecture, including large temples with elaborate <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">stucco</a> façades. Hieroglyphic writing was being used in the Maya region by the 3rd century BC. In the Late Preclassic a number of large cities developed in the <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_Basin" title="Petén Basin">Petén Basin</a>, and the city of <a href="/wiki/Kaminaljuyu" title="Kaminaljuyu">Kaminaljuyu</a> rose to prominence in the <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Highlands" title="Guatemalan Highlands">Guatemalan Highlands</a>. Beginning around 250 AD, the Classic period is largely defined as when the Maya were raising sculpted monuments with <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar" title="Mesoamerican Long Count calendar">Long Count dates</a>. This period saw the Maya civilization develop many <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> linked by a complex <a href="/wiki/Trade_in_Maya_civilization" title="Trade in Maya civilization">trade network</a>. In the Maya Lowlands two great rivals, the cities of <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calakmul" title="Calakmul">Calakmul</a>, became powerful. The Classic period also saw the intrusive intervention of the central Mexican city of <a href="/wiki/Teotihuacan" title="Teotihuacan">Teotihuacan</a> in Maya dynastic politics. In the 9th century, there was a widespread <a href="/wiki/Classic_Maya_collapse" title="Classic Maya collapse">political collapse</a> in the central Maya region, resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internecine_war" title="Internecine war">internecine warfare</a>, the abandonment of cities, and a northward shift of population. The Postclassic period saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza">Chichen Itza</a> in the north, and the expansion of the aggressive <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCiche%CA%BC_kingdom_of_Q%CA%BCumarkaj" title="Kʼicheʼ kingdom of Qʼumarkaj">Kʼicheʼ kingdom</a> in the Guatemalan Highlands. In the 16th century, the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a> colonised the Mesoamerican region, and a lengthy series of campaigns saw the fall of <a href="/wiki/Nojpet%C3%A9n" title="Nojpetén">Nojpetén</a>, the last Maya city, in 1697.
</p><p>Rule during the Classic period centred on the concept of the "divine king", who was thought to act as a mediator between mortals and the supernatural realm. Kingship was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patrilineal" title="Patrilineal">patrilineal</a>, and power normally <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">passed to the eldest son</a>. A prospective king was expected to be a successful war leader as well as a ruler. Closed patronage systems were the dominant force in Maya politics, although how patronage affected the political makeup of a kingdom varied from city-state to city-state. By the Late Classic period, the aristocracy had grown in size, reducing the previously exclusive power of the king. The Maya developed sophisticated art forms using both perishable and non-perishable materials, including wood, <a href="/wiki/Jade_use_in_Mesoamerica" title="Jade use in Mesoamerica">jade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Obsidian_use_in_Mesoamerica" title="Obsidian use in Mesoamerica">obsidian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maya_ceramics" title="Maya ceramics">ceramics</a>, sculpted stone monuments, stucco, and finely painted murals.
</p><p>Maya cities tended to expand organically. The city centers comprised ceremonial and administrative complexes, surrounded by an irregularly shaped sprawl of residential districts. Different parts of a city were often linked by <a href="/wiki/Sacbe" title="Sacbe">causeways</a>. Architecturally, city buildings included palaces, <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_pyramids" title="Mesoamerican pyramids">pyramid-temples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_ballcourt" title="Mesoamerican ballcourt">ceremonial ballcourts</a>, and structures specially aligned for astronomical observation. The Maya elite were literate, and developed a complex system of hieroglyphic writing. Theirs was the most advanced writing system in the pre-Columbian Americas. The Maya recorded their history and ritual knowledge in <a href="/wiki/Maya_codices" title="Maya codices">screenfold books</a>, of which only three uncontested examples remain, the rest having been destroyed by the Spanish. In addition, a great many examples of Maya texts can be found on <a href="/wiki/Maya_stelae" title="Maya stelae">stelae</a> and ceramics. The Maya developed a highly complex series of interlocking ritual calendars, and employed mathematics that included one of the earliest known instances of the explicit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/0_(number)" title="0 (number)">zero</a> in human history. As a part of their religion, the Maya practised <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice_in_Maya_culture" title="Human sacrifice in Maya culture">human sacrifice</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mayamap.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="789" data-file-width="1074" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mayamap.png/220px-Mayamap.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mayamap.png/330px-Mayamap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mayamap.png/440px-Mayamap.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mayamap.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Maya area within <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ceren_(8)_sm_(4290048784).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Ceren_%288%29_sm_%284290048784%29.jpg/220px-Ceren_%288%29_sm_%284290048784%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Ceren_%288%29_sm_%284290048784%29.jpg/330px-Ceren_%288%29_sm_%284290048784%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Ceren_%288%29_sm_%284290048784%29.jpg/440px-Ceren_%288%29_sm_%284290048784%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ceren_(8)_sm_(4290048784).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Remains in <a href="/wiki/Joya_de_Cer%C3%A9n" title="Joya de Cerén">Joya de Cerén</a>, a Classic-era settlement in El Salvador buried under volcanic ash around 600 AD. Its preservation has greatly helped in the study of everyday life in a Maya farming community.</div></div></div>
<p>The Maya civilization developed within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_Region" title="Maya Region">Maya Region</a>, a Mesoamerican cultural area, which covers a region that spreads from northern Mexico southwards into Central America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharer_and_Traxler_2006,_p._28_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sharer_and_Traxler_2006,_p._28-2">[2]</a></sup> Mesoamerica was one of six <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_civilization" title="Cradle of civilization">cradles of civilization</a> worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The Mesoamerican area gave rise to a series of cultural developments that included <a href="/wiki/Complex_society" title="Complex society">complex societies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Mesoamerica" title="Agriculture in Mesoamerica">agriculture</a>, cities, <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_architecture" title="Mesoamerican architecture">monumental architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_writing_systems" title="Mesoamerican writing systems">writing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_calendars" title="Mesoamerican calendars">calendrical systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The set of traits shared by Mesoamerican cultures also included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_astronomy" title="Mesoamerican astronomy">astronomical knowledge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bloodletting_in_Mesoamerica" title="Bloodletting in Mesoamerica">blood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice" title="Human sacrifice">human sacrifice</a>, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_mythology" title="Mesoamerican mythology">cosmovision</a> that viewed the world as divided into four divisions aligned with the <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_direction" title="Cardinal direction">cardinal directions</a>, each with different attributes, and a three-way division of the world into the celestial realm, the earth, and the underworld.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 6000 BC, the early inhabitants of Mesoamerica were experimenting with the domestication of plants, a process that eventually led to the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_society" title="Agrarian society">agricultural societies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The diverse climate allowed for wide variation in available crops, but all regions of Mesoamerica cultivated the base crops of maize, beans, and squashes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> All Mesoamerican cultures used <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_technology" title="Prehistoric technology">Stone Age technology</a>; after c. 1000 AD <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy_in_pre-Columbian_Mesoamerica" title="Metallurgy in pre-Columbian Mesoamerica">copper, silver and gold</a> were worked. Mesoamerica lacked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draft_animals" title="Draft animals">draft animals</a>, did not use the wheel, and possessed few domesticated animals; the principal means of transport was on foot or by canoe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Mesoamericans viewed the world as hostile and governed by unpredictable deities. The <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_ballgame" title="Mesoamerican ballgame">ritual Mesoamerican ballgame</a> was widely played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Mesoamerica is linguistically diverse, with most <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_languages" title="Mesoamerican languages">languages</a> falling within a small number of <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language families</a>—the major families are <a href="/wiki/Mayan_languages" title="Mayan languages">Mayan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mixe%E2%80%93Zoque_languages" title="Mixe–Zoque languages">Mixe–Zoquean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oto-Manguean_languages" title="Oto-Manguean languages">Otomanguean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Uto-Aztecan_languages" title="Uto-Aztecan languages">Uto-Aztecan</a>; there are also a number of smaller families and <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">isolates</a>. The Mesoamerican <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language_area" title="Language area">language area</a> shares a number of important features, including widespread <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>, and use of a <a href="/wiki/Vigesimal" title="Vigesimal">vigesimal</a> number system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The territory of the Maya covered a third of Mesoamerica,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foster02p5_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foster02p5-11">[11]</a></sup> and the Maya were engaged in a dynamic relationship with neighbouring cultures that included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmecs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mixtec" title="Mixtec">Mixtecs</a>, Teotihuacan, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> During the Early Classic period, the Maya cities of Tikal and Kaminaljuyu were key Maya foci in a network that extended beyond the Maya area into the highlands of central Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> At around the same time, there was a strong Maya presence at the Tetitla compound of Teotihuacan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Centuries later, during the 9th century AD, murals at <a href="/wiki/Cacaxtla" title="Cacaxtla">Cacaxtla</a>, another site in the central Mexican highlands, were painted in a Maya style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> This may have been either an effort to align itself with the still-powerful Maya area after the collapse of Teotihuacan and ensuing political fragmentation in the Mexican Highlands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> or an attempt to express a distant Maya origin of the inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The Maya city of Chichen Itza and the distant <a href="/wiki/Toltec" title="Toltec">Toltec</a> capital of <a href="/wiki/Tula_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="Tula (Mesoamerican site)">Tula</a> had an especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya-Toltec_controversy_at_Chichen_Itza" title="Maya-Toltec controversy at Chichen Itza">close relationship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_Region" title="Maya Region">Maya Region</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maya_civilization_location_map_-_geography.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Maya_civilization_location_map_-_geography.svg/220px-Maya_civilization_location_map_-_geography.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Maya_civilization_location_map_-_geography.svg/330px-Maya_civilization_location_map_-_geography.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Maya_civilization_location_map_-_geography.svg/440px-Maya_civilization_location_map_-_geography.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maya_civilization_location_map_-_geography.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maya area</div></div></div>
<p>The Maya civilization occupied a wide territory that included southeastern Mexico and northern Central America. This area included the entire Yucatán Peninsula and all of the territory now incorporated into the modern countries of Guatemala and Belize, as well as the western portions of Honduras and El Salvador.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SharerTraxler06p26_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-SharerTraxler06p26-19">[19]</a></sup> Most of the peninsula is formed by a vast plain with few hills or mountains and a generally low coastline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_1966,_p._25_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_1966,_p._25-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Petén region consists of densely forested low-lying limestone plain;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> a chain of fourteen lakes runs across the central drainage basin of Petén.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> To the south the plain gradually rises towards the Guatemalan Highlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Dense forest covers northern Petén and Belize, most of <a href="/wiki/Quintana_Roo" title="Quintana Roo">Quintana Roo</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Campeche" title="Campeche">Campeche</a>, and a portion of the south of <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n" title="Yucatán">Yucatán</a> state. Farther north, the vegetation turns to lower forest consisting of dense scrub.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Littoral_zone" title="Littoral zone">littoral zone</a> of <a href="/wiki/Soconusco" title="Soconusco">Soconusco</a> lies to the south of the Sierra Madre de Chiapas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and consists of a narrow coastal plain and the foothills of the Sierra Madre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The Maya highlands extend eastwards from Chiapas into Guatemala, reaching their highest in the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_los_Cuchumatanes" title="Sierra de los Cuchumatanes">Sierra de los Cuchumatanes</a>. The major pre-Columbian population centres of the highlands were located in the largest highland valleys, such as the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_Guatemala" title="Valley of Guatemala">Valley of Guatemala</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Quetzaltenango" title="Quetzaltenango">Quetzaltenango</a> Valley. In the southern highlands, a belt of volcanic cones runs parallel to the Pacific coast. The highlands extend northwards into <a href="/wiki/Verapaz,_Guatemala" title="Verapaz, Guatemala">Verapaz</a>, and gradually descend to the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Maya_civilization" title="History of the Maya civilization">History of the Maya civilization</a></div>
<p>The history of Maya civilization is divided into three principal periods: the Preclassic, Classic, and Postclassic periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> These were preceded by the Archaic Period, during which the first settled villages and early developments in agriculture emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Modern scholars regard these periods as arbitrary divisions of Maya chronology, rather than indicative of cultural evolution or decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Definitions of the start and end dates of period spans can vary by as much as a century, depending on the author.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span id="Preclassic_period_.28c._2000_BC_.E2.80.93_250_AD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Preclassic_period_(c._2000_BC_–_250_AD)">Preclassic period (c. 2000 BC – 250 AD)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Preclassic_Maya" title="Preclassic Maya">Preclassic Maya</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:430px;max-width:430px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaminaljuyu_7.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Kaminaljuyu_7.jpg/200px-Kaminaljuyu_7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Kaminaljuyu_7.jpg/300px-Kaminaljuyu_7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Kaminaljuyu_7.jpg/400px-Kaminaljuyu_7.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:El_Mirador_5.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/El_Mirador_5.jpg/222px-El_Mirador_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/El_Mirador_5.jpg/333px-El_Mirador_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/El_Mirador_5.jpg/444px-El_Mirador_5.jpg 2x" width="222"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Kaminaljuyu" title="Kaminaljuyu">Kaminaljuyu</a>, in the highlands, and <a href="/wiki/El_Mirador" title="El Mirador">El Mirador</a>, in the lowlands, were both important cities in the Late Preclassic.</div></div></div></div>
<p>The Maya developed their first civilization in the Preclassic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Scholars continue to discuss when this era of Maya civilization began. Maya occupation at <a href="/wiki/Cuello" title="Cuello">Cuello</a> (modern-day Belize) has been carbon dated to around 2600 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Settlements were established around 1800 BC in the Soconusco region of the Pacific coast, and the Maya were already cultivating the staple crops of maize, beans, squash, and chili pepper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drew99p6_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drew99p6-37">[37]</a></sup> This period was characterised by <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentary</a> communities and the introduction of pottery and fired clay figurines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology" title="Mesoamerican chronology">Middle Preclassic Period</a>, small villages began to grow to form cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olmedo97p26_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olmedo97p26-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nakbe" title="Nakbe">Nakbe</a> in the Petén department of Guatemala is the earliest well-documented city in the Maya lowlands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SharerTraxler06p214_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-SharerTraxler06p214-40">[40]</a></sup> where large structures have been dated to around 750 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olmedo97p26_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Olmedo97p26-39">[39]</a></sup> The northern lowlands of Yucatán were widely settled by the Middle Preclassic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> By approximately 400 BC, early Maya rulers were raising stelae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> A developed script was already being used in Petén by the 3rd century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturno2006_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saturno2006-43">[43]</a></sup> In the Late Preclassic Period, the enormous city of <a href="/wiki/El_Mirador" title="El Mirador">El Mirador</a> grew to cover approximately 16 square kilometres (6.2 sq mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olmedo97p28_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olmedo97p28-44">[44]</a></sup> Although not as large, Tikal was already a significant city by around 350 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MartinGrube00p25-26_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-MartinGrube00p25-26-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the highlands, <a href="/wiki/Kaminaljuyu" title="Kaminaljuyu">Kaminaljuyu</a> emerged as a principal centre in the Late Preclassic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Takalik_Abaj" title="Takalik Abaj">Takalik Abaj</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chocol%C3%A1" title="Chocolá">Chocolá</a> were two of the most important cities on the Pacific coastal plain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Komchen" title="Komchen">Komchen</a> grew to become an important site in northern Yucatán.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The Late Preclassic cultural florescence collapsed in the 1st century AD and many of the great Maya cities of the epoch were abandoned; the cause of this collapse is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MartinGrube00p8_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-MartinGrube00p8-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Classic_period_.28c._250.E2.80.93900_AD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classic_period_(c._250–900_AD)">Classic period (c. 250–900 AD)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:QuiriguaStela1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/QuiriguaStela1.jpg/170px-QuiriguaStela1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/QuiriguaStela1.jpg/255px-QuiriguaStela1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/QuiriguaStela1.jpg/340px-QuiriguaStela1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:QuiriguaStela1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stela D from <a href="/wiki/Quirigu%C3%A1" title="Quiriguá">Quiriguá</a>, representing king <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCak%CA%BC_Tiliw_Chan_Yopaat" title="Kʼakʼ Tiliw Chan Yopaat">Kʼakʼ Tiliw Chan Yopaat</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Classic period is largely defined as the period during which the lowland Maya raised dated monuments using the Long Count calendar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coe_1999,_p._81_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coe_1999,_p._81-51">[51]</a></sup> This period marked the peak of large-scale construction and <a href="/wiki/Urbanism" title="Urbanism">urbanism</a>, the recording of monumental inscriptions, and demonstrated significant intellectual and artistic development, particularly in the southern lowland regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coe_1999,_p._81_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coe_1999,_p._81-51">[51]</a></sup> The Classic period Maya political landscape has been likened to that of <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Renaissance Italy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical Greece</a>, with multiple city-states engaged in a complex network of alliances and enmities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The largest cities had populations numbering 50,000 to 120,000 and were linked to networks of subsidiary sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masson_2012,_p._18237_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Masson_2012,_p._18237-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Early Classic, cities throughout the Maya region were influenced by the great metropolis of <a href="/wiki/Teotihuacan" title="Teotihuacan">Teotihuacan</a> in the distant <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_Mexico" title="Valley of Mexico">Valley of Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MartinGrube00p9_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-MartinGrube00p9-54">[54]</a></sup> In AD 378, Teotihuacan decisively intervened at Tikal and other nearby cities, deposed their rulers, and installed a new Teotihuacan-backed dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> This intervention was led by <a href="/wiki/Siyaj_K%CA%BCak%CA%BC" title="Siyaj Kʼakʼ">Siyaj Kʼakʼ</a> ("Born of Fire"), who arrived at Tikal in early 378. The king of Tikal, <a href="/wiki/Chak_Tok_Ich%CA%BCaak_I" title="Chak Tok Ichʼaak I">Chak Tok Ichʼaak I</a>, died on the same day, suggesting a violent takeover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> A year later, Siyaj Kʼakʼ oversaw the installation of a new king, <a href="/wiki/Yax_Nuun_Ahiin_I" title="Yax Nuun Ahiin I">Yax Nuun Ahiin I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharer_and_Traxler_2006,_p._324_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sharer_and_Traxler_2006,_p._324-57">[57]</a></sup> The installation of the new dynasty led to a period of political dominance when Tikal became the most powerful city in the central lowlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharer_and_Traxler_2006,_p._324_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sharer_and_Traxler_2006,_p._324-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tikal's great rival was Calakmul, another powerful city in the Petén Basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olmeda97p36_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olmeda97p36-58">[58]</a></sup> Tikal and Calakmul both developed extensive systems of allies and vassals; lesser cities that entered one of these networks gained prestige from their association with the top-tier city, and maintained peaceful relations with other members of the same network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Tikal and Calakmul engaged in the manoeuvering of their alliance networks against each other. At various points during the Classic period, one or other of these powers would gain a strategic victory over its great rival, resulting in respective periods of florescence and decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calakmul_-_Structure_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Calakmul_-_Structure_I.jpg/220px-Calakmul_-_Structure_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Calakmul_-_Structure_I.jpg/330px-Calakmul_-_Structure_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Calakmul_-_Structure_I.jpg/440px-Calakmul_-_Structure_I.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calakmul_-_Structure_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Calakmul" title="Calakmul">Calakmul</a> was one of the most important Classic period cities.</div></div></div>
<p>In 629, <a href="/wiki/B%CA%BCalaj_Chan_K%CA%BCawiil" title="Bʼalaj Chan Kʼawiil">Bʼalaj Chan Kʼawiil</a>, a son of the Tikal king <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=K%CA%BCinich_Muwaan_Jol_II&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kʼinich Muwaan Jol II (page does not exist)">Kʼinich Muwaan Jol II</a>, was sent to found a new city at <a href="/wiki/Dos_Pilas" title="Dos Pilas">Dos Pilas</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Petexbat%C3%BAn_Lake" title="Petexbatún Lake">Petexbatún</a> region, apparently as an outpost to extend Tikal's power beyond the reach of Calakmul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> For the next two decades he fought loyally for his brother and overlord at Tikal. In 648, king <a href="/wiki/Yuknoom_Ch%CA%BCeen_II" title="Yuknoom Chʼeen II">Yuknoom Chʼeen II</a> of Calakmul captured Balaj Chan Kʼawiil. Yuknoom Chʼeen II then reinstated Balaj Chan Kʼawiil upon the throne of Dos Pilas as his vassal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> He thereafter served as a loyal ally of Calakmul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the southeast, <a href="/wiki/Cop%C3%A1n" title="Copán">Copán</a> was the most important city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olmeda97p36_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Olmeda97p36-58">[58]</a></sup> Its Classic-period dynasty was founded in 426 by <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCinich_Yax_K%CA%BCuk%CA%BC_Mo%CA%BC" title="Kʼinich Yax Kʼukʼ Moʼ">Kʼinich Yax Kʼukʼ Moʼ</a>. The new king had strong ties with central Petén and Teotihuacan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Copán reached the height of its cultural and artistic development during the rule of <a href="/wiki/Uaxaclajuun_Ub%CA%BCaah_K%CA%BCawiil" title="Uaxaclajuun Ubʼaah Kʼawiil">Uaxaclajuun Ubʼaah Kʼawiil</a>, who ruled from 695 to 738.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> His reign ended catastrophically when he was captured by his vassal, king <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCak%CA%BC_Tiliw_Chan_Yopaat" title="Kʼakʼ Tiliw Chan Yopaat">Kʼakʼ Tiliw Chan Yopaat</a> of <a href="/wiki/Quirigu%C3%A1" title="Quiriguá">Quiriguá</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The captured lord of Copán was taken back to Quiriguá and was decapitated in a public ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-67">[67]</a></sup> It is likely that this coup was backed by Calakmul, in order to weaken a powerful ally of Tikal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">Palenque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yaxchilan" title="Yaxchilan">Yaxchilan</a> were the most powerful cities in the <a href="/wiki/Usumacinta_River" title="Usumacinta River">Usumacinta</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olmeda97p36_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Olmeda97p36-58">[58]</a></sup> In the highlands, <a href="/wiki/Kaminaljuyu" title="Kaminaljuyu">Kaminaljuyu</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_Guatemala" title="Valley of Guatemala">Valley of Guatemala</a> was already a sprawling city by 300.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In the north of the Maya area, <a href="/wiki/Coba" title="Coba">Coba</a> was the most important capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SharerTraxler06p554_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-SharerTraxler06p554-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Classic_Maya_collapse">Classic Maya collapse</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classic_Maya_collapse" title="Classic Maya collapse">Classic Maya collapse</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chichen_Itza_ruins_in_Mexico_--_by_John_Romkey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Chichen_Itza_ruins_in_Mexico_--_by_John_Romkey.jpg/220px-Chichen_Itza_ruins_in_Mexico_--_by_John_Romkey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Chichen_Itza_ruins_in_Mexico_--_by_John_Romkey.jpg/330px-Chichen_Itza_ruins_in_Mexico_--_by_John_Romkey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Chichen_Itza_ruins_in_Mexico_--_by_John_Romkey.jpg/440px-Chichen_Itza_ruins_in_Mexico_--_by_John_Romkey.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chichen_Itza_ruins_in_Mexico_--_by_John_Romkey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza">Chichen Itza</a> was the most important city in the northern Maya region.</div></div></div>
<p>During the 9th century AD, the central Maya region suffered major political collapse, marked by the abandonment of cities, the ending of dynasties, and a northward shift in activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MartinGrube00p9_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-MartinGrube00p9-54">[54]</a></sup> No universally accepted theory explains this collapse, but it likely had a combination of causes, including endemic internecine warfare, overpopulation resulting in severe <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">environmental degradation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coe_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-coe-71">[71]</a></sup> During this period, known as the Terminal Classic, the northern cities of Chichen Itza and <a href="/wiki/Uxmal" title="Uxmal">Uxmal</a> showed increased activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MartinGrube00p9_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-MartinGrube00p9-54">[54]</a></sup> Major cities in the northern Yucatán Peninsula continued to be inhabited long after the cities of the southern lowlands ceased to raise monuments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Classic Maya social organization was based on the ritual authority of the ruler, rather than central control of trade and food distribution. This model of rulership was poorly structured to respond to changes, because the ruler's actions were limited by <a href="/wiki/Traditional_society" title="Traditional society">tradition</a> to such activities as construction, ritual, and warfare. This only served to exacerbate systemic problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> By the 9th and 10th centuries, this resulted in collapse of this system of rulership. In the northern Yucatán, individual rule was replaced by a ruling council formed from elite lineages. In the southern Yucatán and central Petén, kingdoms declined; in western Petén and some other areas, the changes were catastrophic and resulted in the rapid depopulation of cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Within a couple of generations, large swathes of the central Maya area were all but abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Both the capitals and their secondary centres were generally abandoned within a period of 50 to 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masson_2012,_p._18237_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Masson_2012,_p._18237-53">[53]</a></sup> One by one, cities stopped sculpting dated monuments; the last Long Count date was inscribed at <a href="/wiki/Tonin%C3%A1" title="Toniná">Toniná</a> in 909. Stelae were no longer raised, and squatters moved into abandoned royal palaces. Mesoamerican trade routes shifted and bypassed Petén.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Postclassic_period_.28c._950.E2.80.931539_AD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Postclassic_period_(c._950–1539_AD)">Postclassic period (c. 950–1539 AD)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/League_of_Mayapan" title="League of Mayapan">League of Mayapan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zacuelu2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3031" data-file-width="4664" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Zacuelu2.jpg/220px-Zacuelu2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Zacuelu2.jpg/330px-Zacuelu2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Zacuelu2.jpg/440px-Zacuelu2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zacuelu2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zaculeu" title="Zaculeu">Zaculeu</a> was capital of the Postclassic <a href="/wiki/Mam_people" title="Mam people">Mam</a> kingdom in the <a href="/wiki/Guatemalan_Highlands" title="Guatemalan Highlands">Guatemalan Highlands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Although much reduced, a significant Maya presence remained into the Postclassic period after the abandonment of the major Classic period cities; the population was particularly concentrated near permanent water sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Unlike during previous cycles of contraction in the Maya region, abandoned lands were not quickly resettled in the Postclassic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masson_2012,_p._18237_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Masson_2012,_p._18237-53">[53]</a></sup> Activity shifted to the northern lowlands and the Maya Highlands; this may have involved migration from the southern lowlands, because many Postclassic Maya groups had migration myths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Chichen Itza and its <a href="/wiki/Puuc" title="Puuc">Puuc</a> neighbours declined dramatically in the 11th century, and this may represent the final episode of Classic Period collapse. After the decline of Chichen Itza, the Maya region lacked a dominant power until the rise of the city of <a href="/wiki/Mayapan" title="Mayapan">Mayapan</a> in the 12th century. New cities arose near the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf</a> coasts, and new trade networks were formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masson2012p18238_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Masson2012p18238-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Postclassic Period was marked by changes from the preceding Classic Period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arroyo01p38_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arroyo01p38-81">[81]</a></sup> The once-great city of Kaminaljuyu in the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_Guatemala" title="Valley of Guatemala">Valley of Guatemala</a> was abandoned after continuous occupation of almost 2,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SharerTraxler06p618_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-SharerTraxler06p618-82">[82]</a></sup> Across the highlands and neighbouring Pacific coast, long-occupied cities in exposed locations were relocated, apparently due to a proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Maya_warfare" title="Maya warfare">warfare</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Cities came to occupy more-easily defended hilltop locations surrounded by deep ravines, with ditch-and-wall defences sometimes supplementing the protection provided by the natural terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SharerTraxler06p618_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-SharerTraxler06p618-82">[82]</a></sup> One of the most important cities in the Guatemalan Highlands at this time was <a href="/wiki/Q%CA%BCumarkaj" title="Qʼumarkaj">Qʼumarkaj</a>, the capital of the aggressive <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCiche%CA%BC_kingdom_of_Q%CA%BCumarkaj" title="Kʼicheʼ kingdom of Qʼumarkaj">Kʼicheʼ kingdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arroyo01p38_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arroyo01p38-81">[81]</a></sup> The government of Maya states, from the Yucatán to the Guatemalan highlands, was often organised as joint rule by a council. However, in practice one member of the council could act as a supreme ruler, while the other members served him as advisors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mayapan_chac.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Mayapan_chac.JPG/220px-Mayapan_chac.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Mayapan_chac.JPG/330px-Mayapan_chac.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Mayapan_chac.JPG/440px-Mayapan_chac.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mayapan_chac.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mayapan" title="Mayapan">Mayapan</a> was an important Postclassic city in the northern <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Mayapan was abandoned around 1448, after a period of political, social and environmental turbulence that in many ways echoed the Classic period collapse in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Maya_area" title="Southern Maya area">southern Maya region</a>. The abandonment of the city was followed by a period of prolonged warfare, disease and natural disasters in the Yucatán Peninsula, which ended only shortly before Spanish contact in 1511.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Even without a dominant regional capital, the early Spanish explorers reported wealthy coastal cities and thriving marketplaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masson2012p18238_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Masson2012p18238-80">[80]</a></sup> During the Late Postclassic, the Yucatán Peninsula was divided into a number of independent provinces that shared a common culture but varied in internal sociopolitical organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> On the eve of the Spanish conquest, the highlands of Guatemala were dominated by several powerful Maya states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCiche%CA%BC_people" title="Kʼicheʼ people">Kʼicheʼ</a> had carved out a small empire covering a large part of the western Guatemalan Highlands and the neighbouring Pacific coastal plain. However, in the decades before the Spanish conquest of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaqchikel_kingdom" title="Kaqchikel kingdom">Kaqchikel kingdom</a> had been steadily eroding the kingdom of the Kʼicheʼ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Contact_period_and_Spanish_conquest_.281511.E2.80.931697_AD.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Contact_period_and_Spanish_conquest_(1511–1697_AD)">Contact period and Spanish conquest (1511–1697 AD)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Maya" title="Spanish conquest of the Maya">Spanish conquest of the Maya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Chiapas" title="Spanish conquest of Chiapas">Chiapas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Guatemala" title="Spanish conquest of Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Pet%C3%A9n" title="Spanish conquest of Petén">Petén</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Yucat%C3%A1n" title="Spanish conquest of Yucatán">Yucatán</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Honduras" title="Spanish conquest of Honduras">Spanish conquest of Honduras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Aztec_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Aztec Empire">Spanish conquest of the Aztec Empire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish colonization of the Americas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala_Iximche.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="584" data-file-width="674" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala_Iximche.gif/220px-Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala_Iximche.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala_Iximche.gif/330px-Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala_Iximche.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala_Iximche.gif/440px-Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala_Iximche.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala_Iximche.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page from the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lienzo_de_Tlaxcala" title="Lienzo de Tlaxcala">Lienzo de Tlaxcala</a></i> showing the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Guatemala" title="Spanish conquest of Guatemala">Spanish conquest</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iximche" title="Iximche">Iximche</a>, known as Cuahtemallan in the <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> language</div></div></div>
<p>In 1511, a Spanish <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">caravel</a> was wrecked in the Caribbean, and about a dozen survivors made landfall on the coast of Yucatán. They were seized by a Maya lord, and most were <a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice_in_Maya_culture" title="Human sacrifice in Maya culture">sacrificed</a>, although two managed to escape. From 1517 to 1519, three separate Spanish expeditions explored the Yucatán coast, and engaged in a number of battles with the Maya inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> After the Aztec capital <a href="/wiki/Tenochtitlan" title="Tenochtitlan">Tenochtitlan</a> fell to the Spanish in 1521, <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Hernán Cortés</a> despatched <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Alvarado" title="Pedro de Alvarado">Pedro de Alvarado</a> to Guatemala with 180 cavalry, 300 infantry, 4 cannons, and thousands of allied warriors from central Mexico;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> they arrived in Soconusco in 1523.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The Kʼicheʼ capital, Qʼumarkaj, fell to Alvarado in 1524.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Shortly afterwards, the Spanish were invited as allies into <a href="/wiki/Iximche" title="Iximche">Iximche</a>, the capital city of the Kaqchikel Maya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Good relations did not last, due to excessive Spanish demands for gold as tribute, and the city was abandoned a few months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> This was followed by the fall of <a href="/wiki/Zaculeu" title="Zaculeu">Zaculeu</a>, the Mam Maya capital, in 1525.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Montejo" title="Francisco de Montejo">Francisco de Montejo</a> and his son, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Montejo_the_Younger" title="Francisco de Montejo the Younger">Francisco de Montejo the Younger</a>, launched a long series of campaigns against the polities of the Yucatán Peninsula in 1527, and finally completed the conquest of the northern portion of the peninsula in 1546.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> This left only the Maya kingdoms of the Petén Basin independent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> In 1697, <a href="/wiki/Mart%C3%ADn_de_Urs%C3%BAa" title="Martín de Ursúa">Martín de Ursúa</a> launched an assault on the <a href="/wiki/Itza_people" title="Itza people">Itza</a> capital <a href="/wiki/Nojpet%C3%A9n" title="Nojpetén">Nojpetén</a> and the last independent Maya city fell to the Spanish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Persistence_of_Maya_culture">Persistence of Maya culture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Maya_peoples" title="Maya peoples">Maya peoples</a></div>
<p>The Spanish conquest stripped away most of the defining features of Maya civilization. However, many Maya villages remained remote from Spanish colonial authority, and for the most part continued to manage their own affairs. Maya communities and the nuclear family maintained their traditional day-to-day life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The basic Mesoamerican diet of maize and beans continued, although agricultural output was improved by the introduction of steel tools. Traditional crafts such as weaving, ceramics, and basketry continued to be practised. Community markets and trade in local products continued long after the conquest. At times, the colonial administration encouraged the traditional economy in order to extract tribute in the form of ceramics or cotton textiles, although these were usually made to European specifications. Maya beliefs and language proved resistant to change, despite vigorous efforts by <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> missionaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The 260-day <i><a href="/wiki/Tzolk%CA%BCin" title="Tzolkʼin">tzolkʼin</a></i> ritual calendar continues in use in modern Maya communities in the highlands of Guatemala and Chiapas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and millions of Mayan-language speakers inhabit the territory in which their ancestors developed their civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investigation_of_Maya_civilization">Investigation of Maya civilization</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uxmal_nunnery_by_Catherwood_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1078" data-file-width="1408" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Uxmal_nunnery_by_Catherwood_02.jpg/220px-Uxmal_nunnery_by_Catherwood_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Uxmal_nunnery_by_Catherwood_02.jpg/330px-Uxmal_nunnery_by_Catherwood_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Uxmal_nunnery_by_Catherwood_02.jpg/440px-Uxmal_nunnery_by_Catherwood_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uxmal_nunnery_by_Catherwood_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drawing by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Catherwood" title="Frederick Catherwood">Frederick Catherwood</a> of the Nunnery complex at <a href="/wiki/Uxmal" title="Uxmal">Uxmal</a></div></div></div>
<p>The agents of the Catholic Church wrote detailed accounts of the Maya, in support of their efforts at <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianization</a>, and absorption of the Maya into the Spanish Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demarest04p31_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demarest04p31-102">[102]</a></sup> This was followed by various Spanish priests and colonial officials who left descriptions of ruins they visited in Yucatán and Central America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In 1839, American traveller and writer <a href="/wiki/John_Lloyd_Stephens" title="John Lloyd Stephens">John Lloyd Stephens</a> set out to visit a number of Maya sites with English architect and draftsman <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Catherwood" title="Frederick Catherwood">Frederick Catherwood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Their illustrated accounts of the ruins sparked strong popular interest, and brought the Maya to the attention of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demarest04p31_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Demarest04p31-102">[102]</a></sup> The later 19th century saw the recording and recovery of ethnohistoric accounts of the Maya, and the first steps in deciphering Maya hieroglyphs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>The final two decades of the 19th century saw the birth of modern scientific archaeology in the Maya region, with the meticulous work of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Maudslay" title="Alfred Maudslay">Alfred Maudslay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teoberto_Maler" title="Teoberto Maler">Teoberto Maler</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> By the early 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Museum_of_Archaeology_and_Ethnology" title="Peabody Museum of Archaeology and Ethnology">Peabody Museum</a> was sponsoring excavations at Copán and in the Yucatán Peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demarest_2004,_p._38_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demarest_2004,_p._38-107">[107]</a></sup> In the first two decades of the 20th century, advances were made in deciphering the Maya calendar, and identifying deities, dates, and religious concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Since the 1930s, archaeological exploration increased dramatically, with large-scale excavations across the Maya region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Castillo_Maler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1963" data-file-width="2730" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Castillo_Maler.jpg/220px-Castillo_Maler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Castillo_Maler.jpg/330px-Castillo_Maler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Castillo_Maler.jpg/440px-Castillo_Maler.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Castillo_Maler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1892 photograph of the <a href="/wiki/El_Castillo,_Chichen_Itza" title="El Castillo, Chichen Itza">Castillo</a> at <a href="/wiki/Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza">Chichen Itza</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Teoberto_Maler" title="Teoberto Maler">Teoberto Maler</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the 1960s, the distinguished Mayanist <a href="/wiki/J._Eric_S._Thompson" title="J. Eric S. Thompson">J. Eric S. Thompson</a> promoted the ideas that Maya cities were essentially vacant ceremonial centres serving a dispersed population in the forest, and that the Maya civilization was governed by peaceful astronomer-priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Demarest_2004,_p._44_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Demarest_2004,_p._44-110">[110]</a></sup> These ideas began to collapse with major advances in the decipherment of the script in the late 20th century, pioneered by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Heinrich_Berlin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Heinrich Berlin (page does not exist)">Heinrich Berlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatiana_Proskouriakoff" title="Tatiana Proskouriakoff">Tatiana Proskouriakoff</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Knorozov" title="Yuri Knorozov">Yuri Knorozov</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> With breakthroughs in understanding of Maya script since the 1950s, the texts revealed the warlike activities of the Classic Maya kings, and the view of the Maya as peaceful could no longer be supported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2>
<p>Unlike the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca</a>, the Maya political system never integrated the entire Maya cultural area into a single state or empire. Rather, throughout its history, the Maya area contained a varying mix of political complexity that included both <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">states</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chiefdom" title="Chiefdom">chiefdoms</a>. These polities fluctuated greatly in their relationships with each other and were engaged in a complex web of rivalries, periods of dominance or submission, vassalage, and alliances. At times, different polities achieved regional dominance, such as Calakmul, <a href="/wiki/Caracol" title="Caracol">Caracol</a>, Mayapan, and Tikal. The first reliably evidenced polities formed in the Maya lowlands in the 9th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Late Preclassic, the Maya political system coalesced into a <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">theopolitical</a> form, where elite ideology justified the ruler's authority, and was reinforced by public display, ritual, and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The divine king was the centre of political power, exercising ultimate control over the administrative, economic, judicial, and military functions of the polity. The divine authority invested within the ruler was such that the king was able to mobilize both the aristocracy and commoners in executing huge infrastructure projects, apparently with no police force or standing army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Some polities engaged in a strategy of increasing administration, and filling administrative posts with loyal supporters rather than blood relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Within a polity, mid-ranking population centres would have played a key role in managing resources and internal conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Maya political landscape was highly complex and Maya elites engaged in political intrigue to gain economic and social advantage over neighbours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In the Late Classic, some cities established a long period of dominance over other large cities, such as the dominance of Caracol over <a href="/wiki/Naranjo" title="Naranjo">Naranjo</a> for half a century. In other cases, loose alliance networks were formed around a dominant city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Border settlements, usually located about halfway between neighbouring capitals, often switched allegiance over the course of their history, and at times acted independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Dominant capitals exacted tribute in the form of luxury items from subjugated population centres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foias14p161_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foias14p161-121">[121]</a></sup> Political power was reinforced by military power, and the capture and humiliation of enemy warriors played an important part in elite culture. An overriding sense of pride and honour among the warrior aristocracy could lead to extended feuds and vendettas, which caused political instability and the fragmentation of polities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Maya_society" title="Maya society">Maya society</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Maya_society" title="Women in Maya society">Women in Maya society</a></div>
<p>From the Early Preclassic, Maya society was sharply divided between the elite and commoners. As population increased over time, various sectors of society became increasingly specialised, and political organization became increasingly complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> By the Late Classic, when populations had grown enormously and hundreds of cities were connected in a complex web of political hierarchies, the wealthy segment of society multiplied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> A middle class may have developed that included artisans, low ranking <a href="/wiki/Maya_priesthood" title="Maya priesthood">priests</a> and officials, merchants, and soldiers. Commoners included farmers, servants, labourers, and slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> According to indigenous histories, land was held communally by noble houses or <a href="/wiki/Clan" title="Clan">clans</a>. Such clans held that the land was the property of the clan ancestors, and such ties between the land and the ancestors were reinforced by the burial of the dead within residential compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="King_and_court">King and court</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tonin%C3%A1_Stela_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Tonin%C3%A1_Stela_1.jpg/220px-Tonin%C3%A1_Stela_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Tonin%C3%A1_Stela_1.jpg/330px-Tonin%C3%A1_Stela_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Tonin%C3%A1_Stela_1.jpg/440px-Tonin%C3%A1_Stela_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tonin%C3%A1_Stela_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Maya_stelae" title="Maya stelae">Stela</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tonin%C3%A1" title="Toniná">Toniná</a>, representing the 6th-century king <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bahlam_Yaxuun_Tihl&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bahlam Yaxuun Tihl (page does not exist)">Bahlam Yaxuun Tihl</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Classic Maya rule was centred in a royal culture that was displayed in all areas of Classic Maya art. The king was the supreme ruler and held a semi-divine status that made him the mediator between the mortal realm and that of the gods. From very early times, kings were specifically identified with the <a href="/wiki/Maya_maize_god" title="Maya maize god">young maize god</a>, whose gift of maize was the basis of Mesoamerican civilization. Maya royal succession was <a href="/wiki/Patrilineality" title="Patrilineality">patrilineal</a>, and royal power only passed to <a href="/wiki/Women_rulers_in_Maya_society" title="Women rulers in Maya society">queens</a> when doing otherwise would result in the extinction of the dynasty. Typically, power was passed to the eldest son. A young prince was called a <i>chʼok</i> ("youth"), although this word later came to refer to nobility in general. The royal heir was called <i>bʼaah chʼok</i> ("head youth"). Various points in the young prince's <a href="/wiki/Childhood_in_Maya_society" title="Childhood in Maya society">childhood</a> were marked by ritual; the most important was a bloodletting ceremony at age five or six years. Although being of the royal bloodline was of utmost importance, the heir also had to be a successful war leader, as demonstrated by taking of captives. The enthronement of a new king was a highly elaborate ceremony, involving a series of separate acts that included enthronement upon a <a href="/wiki/Jaguars_in_Mesoamerican_cultures" title="Jaguars in Mesoamerican cultures">jaguar-skin</a> cushion, human sacrifice, and receiving the symbols of royal power, such as a headband bearing a jade representation of the so-called "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Maya_jester_god&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Maya jester god (page does not exist)">jester god</a>", an elaborate headdress adorned with <a href="/wiki/Quetzal" title="Quetzal">quetzal</a> feathers, and a sceptre representing the god <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCawiil" title="Kʼawiil">Kʼawiil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>Maya political administration, based around the royal court, was not bureaucratic in nature. Government was hierarchical, and official posts were sponsored by higher-ranking members of the aristocracy; officials tended to be promoted to higher levels of office during the course of their lives. Officials are referred to as being "owned" by their sponsor, and this relationship continued even after the death of the sponsor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foias14p224_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foias14p224-129">[129]</a></sup> The Maya royal court was a vibrant and dynamic political institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> There was no universal structure for the Maya royal court, instead each polity formed a royal court that was suited to its own individual context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> A number of royal and noble titles have been identified by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigrapher" title="Epigrapher">epigraphers</a> translating Classic Maya inscriptions. <i><a href="/wiki/Ajaw" title="Ajaw">Ajaw</a></i> is usually translated as "lord" or "king". In the Early Classic, an <i>ajaw</i> was the ruler of a city. Later, with increasing social complexity, the <i>ajaw</i> was a member of the ruling class and a major city could have more than one, each ruling over different districts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'ArcyHarrison03p114_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-D'ArcyHarrison03p114-132">[132]</a></sup> Paramount rulers distinguished themselves from the extended nobility by prefixing the word <i>kʼuhul</i> to their <i>ajaw</i> title. A <i>kʼuhul ajaw</i> was "divine lord", originally confined to the kings of the most prestigious and ancient royal lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> <i>Kalomte</i> was a royal title, whose exact meaning is not yet deciphered, but it was held only by the most powerful kings of the strongest dynasties. It indicated an overlord, or <a href="/wiki/High_king" title="High king">high king</a>, and the title was only in use during the Classic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> By the Late Classic, the absolute power of the <i>kʼuhul ajaw</i> had weakened, and the political system had diversified to include a wider aristocracy, that by this time may well have expanded disproportionately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maya_Presentation_of_Captives_Kimbell.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2529" data-file-width="2068" decoding="async" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Maya_Presentation_of_Captives_Kimbell.jpg/220px-Maya_Presentation_of_Captives_Kimbell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Maya_Presentation_of_Captives_Kimbell.jpg/330px-Maya_Presentation_of_Captives_Kimbell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Maya_Presentation_of_Captives_Kimbell.jpg/440px-Maya_Presentation_of_Captives_Kimbell.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maya_Presentation_of_Captives_Kimbell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Classic period sculpture showing <i>sajal</i> Aj Chak Maax presenting captives before ruler <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Itzamnaaj_B%CA%BCalam_III&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Itzamnaaj Bʼalam III (page does not exist)">Itzamnaaj Bʼalam III</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yaxchilan" title="Yaxchilan">Yaxchilan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A <i>sajal</i> was ranked below the <i>ajaw</i>, and indicated a subservient lord. A <i>sajal</i> would be lord of a second- or third-tier site, answering to an <i>ajaw</i>, who may himself have been subservient to a <i>kalomte</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'ArcyHarrison03p114_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-D'ArcyHarrison03p114-132">[132]</a></sup> A <i>sajal</i> would often be a war captain or regional governor, and inscriptions often link the <i>sajal</i> title to warfare; they are often mentioned as the holders of war captives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> <i>Sajal</i> meant "feared one".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The titles of <i>ah tzʼihb</i> and <i>ah chʼul hun</i> are both related to scribes. The <i>ah tzʼihb</i> was a royal scribe, usually a member of the royal family; the <i>ah chʼul hun</i> was the Keeper of the Holy Books, a title that is closely associated with the <i>ajaw</i> title, indicating that an <i>ajaw</i> always held the <i>ah chʼul hun</i> title simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Other courtly titles, the functions of which are not well understood, were <i>yajaw kʼahk'</i> ("Lord of Fire"), <i>tiʼhuun</i> and <i>ti'sakhuun</i>. These last two may be variations on the same title,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> and Mark Zender has suggested that the holder of this title may have been the spokesman for the ruler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Courtly titles are overwhelmingly male-oriented, and in those relatively rare occasions where they are applied to a woman, they appear to be used as honorifics for female royalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Titled elites were often associated with particular structures in the hieroglyphic inscriptions of Classic period cities, indicating that such office holders either owned that structure, or that the structure was an important focus for their activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> A <i>lakam</i>, or standard-bearer, was possibly the only non-elite post-holder in the royal court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foias14p224_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foias14p224-129">[129]</a></sup> The <i>lakam</i> was only found in larger sites, and they appear to have been responsible for the taxation of local districts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foias14p224_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Foias14p224-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Different factions may have existed in the royal court. The <i>kʼuhul ahaw</i> and his household would have formed the central power-base, but other important groups were the priesthood, the warrior aristocracy, and other aristocratic courtiers. Where ruling councils existed, as at Chichen Itza and Copán, these may have formed an additional faction. Rivalry between different factions would have led to dynamic political institutions as compromises and disagreements were played out. In such a setting, public performance was vital. Such performances included <a href="/wiki/Maya_dance" title="Maya dance">ritual dances</a>, presentation of war captives, offerings of tribute, human sacrifice, and religious ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
